Hayashi's Take: Texans Vote on Combining Saving Money, Winning Prizes

Fed's Dudley Warns Against Overly Aggressive Regulation Rollback

Analysis: Tricky Balancing Act Faces New York Fed Search Committee

First Director of Office of Financial Research to Leave Post

At Bridgewater, a Secret Settlement and a Groping Claim Against Ray Dalio's Protg

Texans Vote on Combining Saving Money, Winning Prizes

Banks used to offer toasters and wall clocks for opening new accounts. Now some financial institutions try to attract customers by making saving money feel like entering the lottery.

When Texans head to the polls Tuesday, they will be asked to approve or disapprove a proposition to allow financial institutions to conduct raffles to promote saving. The Texas Constitution bans lotteries, with some exceptions, including the Texas Lottery.

Proposition 7 would authorize the Texas Legislature to carve out an exception for such products offered by financial institutions. If the measure passes, Texas will join more than a dozen states that allow banks and credit unions to offer chances to win cash prizes to customers who open new accounts and keep adding money to them.

Major Texas dailies, including the Dallas Morning News and the San Antonio Express-News, recommend that voters approve the proposition. "We view this as a useful tool to encourage people to save money and a more fruitful avenue than using, for instance, payday loans for emergency funds," "http://www.mysanantonio.com/opinion/editorials/article/Prop-7-useful-tool-to-encourage-savings-12321289.php".

State lawmakers from both parties earlier this year overwhelmingly approved putting the amendment to a vote.

The idea is that customers of banks and credit unions would get one entry into a raffle for every $25 they deposit into a savings account. The more money they save, the more chances they have to win a prize, until a certain limit.

Such products began to take off after the American Savings Promotion Act, a federal statute, was implemented in 2015 to approve the use of "savings promotion raffle." Experts believe raffles incentivize people with limited means to save more.

Larger banks offer cash incentives of $100 to $500 to customers who open checking and savings accounts. They generally target wealthier customers by setting their minimum deposit amounts high.

Already, credit unions from over a dozen states including Washington, New York and Indiana participate in a program called "http://www.savetowin.org/product-info/faqs". Customers gain one entry into a drawing for each $25 they deposit for chances to win prizes ranging from $25 to $5,000. The program started in Michigan in 2009, as several credit unions there tried to get people to save even at the height of the financial crisis, when many households were facing financial difficulties.

The Texas proposition specifies that the raffle prizes can't be exorbitant, no additional fees can be charged for entry, and interest must be paid on the savings. The institution can require the money to be kept in the savings account for a specified period to qualify for prizes.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Fed's Dudley Warns Against Overly Aggressive Regulation Rollback

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-dudley-warns-against-overly-aggressive-regulation-roll-back-1509988682" President William Dudley cautioned elected leaders Monday not to go too far in rolling back financial sector regulations that were put in place to shore up the banking sector after the financial crisis.

Mr. Dudley was speaking before the Economic Club of New York, on the same day his bank announced his intention to retire in the middle of next year, ahead of the end of his term in January 2019. The official's unexpectedly accelerated retirement plans arrived amid a shake-up in Fed leadership that saw Vice chairman Stanley Fischer leave the bank last month, and a new Fed chair named last week to lead the central bank next year.

In his remarks Monday, Mr. Dudley joined with other officials who have said that while there is some scope to tinker with some of the postcrisis regulatory changes, too much of an overhaul may put the U.S. economy again at risk of another financial crisis. Similarly, Fed governor Jerome Powell, President Donald Trump's nominee to succeed Janet Yellen as Fed chair in February, has largely endorsed the Fed's efforts to strengthen regulations following the financial crisis to make the banking system safer while being open to reconsidering and adjusting them.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/transcript-audience-q-a-with-federal-reserves-william-dudley-1510009318"

Analysis: Tricky Balancing Act Faces New York Fed Search Committee

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/tricky-balancing-act-faces-new-york-fed-search-committee-1510050602" with a keen knowledge of markets and economics, but who isn't too close to Wall Street because he or she will be responsible for regulating some of the world's biggest banks.

This is the balancing act facing a newly formed search committee for the next president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York following the announcement Monday that the current leader, William Dudley, will step down in mid-2018, several months before his term expires in January 2019.

First Director of Office of Financial Research to Leave Post

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-treasurys-first-director-of-office-of-financial-research-to-leave-post-1509987633" plans to leave by year's end, adding to uncertainty for a young agency that faces proposed budget cuts and an inspector general's probe into management and personnel issues.

Richard Berner has been the only director of the OFR, created by the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial law to help fix problems with financial data and to spot potential risks to the economy. It is an arm of the Treasury Department, but has independent funding and a director confirmed to a six-year term, features designed to insulate the department from political maneuvering.

At Bridgewater, a Secret Settlement and a Groping Claim Against Ray Dalio's Protg

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/bridgewater-paid-over-1-million-to-employee-pushed-out-after-relationship-with-dalios-right-hand-man-1510050781" to a woman who was pushed out after engaging in a consensual relationship with top executive Greg Jensen, and shortly after heard from another female employee that Mr. Jensen had groped her buttocks, according to people familiar with the matter.

Mr. Jensen, 43 years old, was directly mentored by Bridgewater founder Ray Dalio, 68, and groomed over two decades to succeed him as leader of the world's largest hedge fund. At the time both incidents were brought to the company's attention roughly three years ago, he was co-chief executive of the firm. He is now Bridgewater's co-chief investment officer, helping oversee around $160 billion in assets and hundreds of employees.

The billionaire Mr. Dalio was personally involved in mediating both matters, people familiar with them said. Both women have since left Bridgewater and are barred by nondisclosure agreements from discussing their experiences publicly, the people said. Mr. Dalio approved a settlement of more than $1 million for the woman who had a relationship with Mr. Jensen, the people said.

CFTC Fines Cargill $10 Million Over Swaps Trades

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/cftc-fines-cargill-10-million-over-swaps-trades-1509998830" fined Cargill Inc. $10 million for improperly valuing swap trades, a move that the regulator said caused the company's revenue to appear higher than it should have been.

The Minnesota-based agribusiness company misled both its trade counterparties and the data repositories where all swap dealers must report their trades, the agency said. The violations date back to 2013, when Cargill first registered as a swap dealer with the CFTC, and the company has "failed to develop systems or procedures to prevent inaccurate communications" since then, the agency said in its order.

Men Linked to 1MDB Financier Failed in Bid to Buy Bank

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/men-linked-to-1mdb-financier-failed-in-bid-to-buy-bank-1510050600" of a multibillion-dollar Malaysian financial scandal launched a bid last year to buy a controlling stake in a bank in Mauritius prompting an investigation by regulators.

The deal took shape in the months after the U.S. Justice Department alleged in a July 2016 civil asset-forfeiture lawsuit that Jho Low, a Malaysian financier, helped divert billions of dollars from state investment fund 1Malaysia Development Bhd. and at times used small banks to help launder the money. Mr. Low has denied wrongdoing.

In late 2016, former Goldman Sachs Group Inc. partner Tim Leissner, who had been the primary Goldman executive to handle the bank's 1MDB account, and Thai businessman Phengphian Laogumnerd, a friend of Mr. Low's, worked together in an effort to buy Qatari-owned Century Banking Corp. of Mauritius.

Mauritius's central bank blocked the transaction and, concerned about a possible connection between Mr. Leissner and the global 1MDB scandal, notified the U.S. Federal Bureau of Investigation about the bid, according to people briefed on the central bank's actions.

WSJ Pro Short: House Poised to Reauthorize Flood-Insurance Program

House Republicans are preparing to bring to the floor as early as this week legislation to reauthorize the federal flood-insurance program for five years. The move comes after the bill's author, House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas), eased aspects of the bill that would have imposed steep penalties on individuals whose homes have repeatedly flooded. The concessions, which make it harder for repeat flood victims to lose their insurance coverage, was part of a deal reached between Mr. Hensarling and House Whip Steve Scalise (R., La.), who had previously opposed the legislation. The bill is expected to clear the House on Wednesday or Thursday, largely along party lines, though it faces an uncertain future in the Senate, where efforts to reauthorize the program have languished.

Equifax Board Continues to Probe Legal Officer's Share-Sales Role

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/equifax-board-continues-to-probe-legal-officers-share-sales-role-1509991844" is continuing to examine the role of the company's chief legal officer in approving share sales by four executives days after suspicious activity was discovered in the company's systems, according to people familiar with the matter.

That inquiry remains open even though the committee, comprised of independent directors, on Friday issued a report exonerating the executives who sold stock. Three of the four executives sold around $1.8 million of company shares combined.

BlackRock pivots to US west coast as part of tech push

Peter Smith 
728 words
6 November 2017
03:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Head of Americas Mark McCombe relocates to San Francisco

BlackRock is pivoting its business to the US west coast, moving Mark McCombe, head of Americas, to San Francisco as part of a broader plan by the world s top asset manager to step up its focus on technology and innovation.

In a recent staff memo, Larry Fink, BlackRock s chief executive, and Rob Kapito, president, said Mr McCombe s move to San Francisco reflected the  critical importance to BlackRock of tapping the innovation taking place on the west coast .

 The region is home to many of our largest clients and is where the velocity of change in asset management and technology is most rapid. That s why we have asked Mark McCombe to take an expanded leadership role,  Mr Fink and Mr Kapito said.

The Americas division that Mr McCombe leads accounts for two-thirds of BlackRock s assets under management and revenues. More than 40 per cent of BlackRock s $5.9tn in client assets are managed from San Francisco, which accounts for a third of group revenue.

After Mr Kapito, Mr McCombe and Mark Wiedman, head of BlackRock s passive investment arm, are considered top contenders to succeed Mr Fink.

 BlackRock understands that technology is what will drive the business in the next five to 10 years. Wiedman and McCombe are tech savvy and the next generation of leaders,  said an executive at a rival asset management group who knows both men.

Amin Rajan, chief executive of Create-Research, a consultancy, said BlackRock s greater focus on San Francisco will help  seal its claim as a technology-driven company that intends to retain its pole position in the global asset industry .

As head of Americas, Mr McCombe is responsible for Canada, Latin America/Iberia, US wealth advisory, and US and Canada defined contribution businesses. As well as many of BlackRock s largest clients, including Calpers and Calstrs, the pension funds, Mr McCombe is also involved in BlackRock s exchange traded fund and institutional client businesses.

Roughly half of the team that manages BlackRock s lucrative iShares business, the world s largest ETF provider, is located in San Francisco, where it was founded. New York-based Mr Wiedman has oversight of iShares.

 iShares is driving the fast growth and is the most important part of the business,  the executive said.  [Mr McCombe] has driven the regional businesses in Asia-Pacific [from 2012 to 2016] and now the Americas. 

San Francisco is also home to BlackRock s systematic active equity (SAE) investment team, the quantitative investment group headed by Jeff Shen and Raffaele Savi. That business is led by Mark Wiseman, head of equities who is also among the contenders to succeed Mr Fink along with Rob Goldstein, the chief operating officer who heads BlackRock s technology push.

 Systematic active equity is the future. It combines the best of actives and passives and may well emerge as the third way of investing,  said Mr Rajan.  Pension investors perceive it as a device for getting alpha at beta risk and beta charges.

 With the advances in artificial intelligence, it will be a game changer by the end of this decade, as asset managers achieve an optimal machine-human interface to generate investible information and actionable insights. 

BlackRock s systematic active equity and systematic fixed income businesses   previously known as scientific active equity and model-based fixed income   have grown assets under management to more than $150bn.

The company said the term  systematic  reflected the evolution of quantitative management since the financial crisis a decade ago.  The new quant utilises unstructured data, natural-language processing and machine learning, among other techniques, to generate alpha and does so alongside more traditional quantitative approaches,  BlackRock said, adding it planned to  expand these solutions to both institutional and retail clients globally .

In 2015, BlackRock acquired San Francisco-based FutureAdvisor, a robo-adviser crafting automated portfolios for investors, in a deal underscoring how technology is reshaping the market for financial advice.

FutureAdvisor, founded by two former Microsoft engineers, is one of a new generation of tech companies offering asset allocation advice to people who cannot afford   or do not feel comfortable visiting   a traditional broker.

Its products are sold to brokers and financial advisers, who in turn offer them to their clients.

Markets
The Artificial-Intelligent Investor: AI Funds Beckon; Investors see more mutual funds and ETFs that make decisions based on cognitive computing

By Bailey McCann 
928 words
6 November 2017
03:07
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Would you let a thinking robot pick stocks for you?

Fund companies are increasingly turning to artificial intelligence to help inform their investment-management decisions. Using AI to manage investments is nothing new for institutional investors some of the world's most high-profile hedge funds including Renaissance Technologies and Bridgewater Associates rely on computers' analysis to make day-to-day trading decisions. But AI-powered investing is making its way to individual investors, as well.

In October, two new high-profile products using artificial intelligence were launched AI Powered Equity ETF (AIEQ) and the Credit Suisse RavenPack Artificial Intelligence Sentiment (AIS) Index.

AIEQ is the first ETF powered by International Business Machines Corp.'s Watson cognitive computing platform and is managed by San Francisco-based EquBot, an investment company with plans to launch several AI-powered ETFs in the future.

Credit Suisse AG joined with New York-based RavenPack on its AI-powered index product. The investment bank says artificial intelligence will be used to make sector-weighting decisions in the index, which tracks the performance of U.S. large-cap stocks. The AIS index is the first AI product for Credit Suisse.

A machine's insight

Both AIEQ and AIS work on the same principle they rely on machines to analyze reams of analyst notes, articles, earnings-call transcripts, regulatory filings and social-media posts on individual companies to gather insights about how they might perform. From there, the software determines the weighting of their respective indexes and trades.

Art Amador, co-founder and chief operating officer at EquBot, says that EquBot's algorithms with the power of Watson will look for undervalued stocks to invest in that have the potential to outperform over time. A human will monitor the decisions to ensure data quality.

AIEQ represents an evolution in the investment process and landscape. Mr. Amador says his company chose to use Watson because the platform works well with investments.

"All AI is not created equal," he says. "We wanted to be able to offer transparency into what stocks were being chosen and why they were being chosen. The EquBot approach to using AI for investing is designed to provide that understanding."

Watson is already being used in financial services and banking for regulatory compliance and financial data analysis. AIEQ is the first ETF powered by Watson.

Mutual-fund managers are also bringing artificial intelligence into the investment process.

In October, AXA Investment Management's Rosenberg Equities announced it was taking steps to integrate AI into its investment products. Fund managers at Rosenberg Equities, which already uses algorithms to run its funds, also plan to use natural-language-processing technology to analyze things like analyst notes, articles and other unstructured data that could have an impact on how stocks perform. Rosenberg says natural-language processing will inform its smart beta and "core plus" strategies, which are designed to beat the performance of benchmark indexes, largely through stock weightings.

According to Michael Kollo, head of research and chief quantitative strategist for Rosenberg Equities at AXA IM, artificial intelligence will also be folded into investment modeling to help fund algorithms predict risk and extreme price movements in specific stocks.

"We already have a significant computing infrastructure in place, so it makes sense for us to take this step and continue to improve our process," Dr. Kollo says.

The bond bots

AI is also creeping into fixed-income funds. New York-based Wavelength Capital Management's Wavelength Interest Rate Neutral Fund (WAVLX) has been using a proprietary artificial-intelligence system to analyze information and make trading decisions about the bond market since 2013. WAVLX is designed to capture positive return from the bond market without being sensitive to interest rates.

"When we started, there wasn't a lot of data available," says Mark Landis, co-founder at Wavelength. "We were training our software using articles, historical patterns, social-media posts a lot of unstructured data. As regulation increased reporting requirements for all asset classes, we've been able to add that information into our analytics platform. The insights are getting better each year."

WAVLX is up 4.56% year-to-date through Oct. 27. The Bloomberg Barclays U.S. Aggregate Bond Index, the benchmark index for the bond market, was up 2.90% for the same period.

People power

Is it all over for humans?

Not quite. With all of these products, even though computers are taking on the role of a hyper-dialed-in portfolio manager, there are still humans standing by, ready to flip the switch if the software gets carried away.

Dr. Kollo, the head of research at Rosenberg Equities, cautions that investors should use that same kind of vigilance when choosing AI-powered funds.

"The technology itself is only a tool, like a shovel or a hammer," Dr. Kollo says. "Success still depends on the skill of the manager running the strategy," he says. "Investors still need to understand the team, what the process is and what the goals are before they invest."

Ms. McCann is a writer in New York. She can be reached at "mailto:reports@wsj.com".

News
BlackRock pivots to American west coast as part of technology push

PETER SMITH 
571 words
6 November 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Europe
1,2
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

BlackRock pivots to American west coast

BlackRock is pivoting its business to the US west coast, moving Mark McCombe, head of Americas, to San Francisco as part of a broader plan by the world's top asset manager to step up its focus on technology and innovation.

In a recent staff memo, Larry Fink, BlackRock's chief executive, and Rob Kapito, president, said Mr McCombe's move to San Francisco reflected the "critical importance to BlackRock of tapping the innovation taking placeonthe west coast".

"The region is home to many of our largest clients and is where the velocity of change in asset management and technology is most rapid. That's why we have asked Mark McCombeto take an expanded leadership role,"Mr Fink and Mr Kapito said.

The Americas division that Mr McCombe leads accounts for twothirds of BlackRock's assets under management and revenues. More than 40 per cent of BlackRock's $5.9tn in client assets are managed from San Francisco, which accounts for a third of grouprevenue.

After Mr Kapito, Mr McCombe and Mark Wiedman, head of Black-Rock's passive investment arm, are considered top contenders to succeed Mr Fink .

"BlackRock understands that technology is what will drive the business in the next five to 10 years. Wiedman and McCombe are tech savvy and the next generation of leaders," said an executive at a rival asset Management group who knowsbothmen.

Amin Rajan, chief executive of Create-Research., a consultancy, said BlackRock's greater focus on San Francisco will help "seal its claim as a technology-driven company that intends to retain its pole position in the global asset industry".

Mr McCombe is responsible for Canada, Latin America/Iberia, US wealth advisory, and US and Canada defined contribution businesses. As well as many of BlackRock's largest clients, including Calpers and Calstrs, the pension funds, Mr McCombe is also involved in Black-Rock's exchange traded fund and institutional client businesses .

continued on page2 continued from page 1 Roughly half of the team that manages BlackRock's lucrative iShares business, the world's largest ETF provider, is located in San Francisco, where it was founded. New York-based Mr Wiedman has oversight of iShares.

"iShares is driving the fast growth and is the most important part of the business," the executive said. "[Mr McCombe] has driven the regional businesses in Asia-Pacific [from 2012 to 2016] and now the Americas."

San Francisco is also home to BlackRock's systematic active equity (SAE) investment team, the quantitative investment group headed by Jeff Shen and Raffaele Savi. That business is led by Mark Wiseman, head of equities who is also among the contenders to succeed Mr Fink along with Rob Goldstein, the chief operating officer who heads BlackRock's technology push.

"Systematic active equity is the future. It combines the best of actives and passives and may well emerge as the third way of investing," said Mr Rajan. "Pension investors perceive it as a device for getting alpha at beta risk and beta charges.

"With the advances in artificial intelligence, it will be a game changer by the end of this decade, as asset managers achieve an optimal machinehuman interface to generate investible information and actionable insights."

BlackRock's systematic active equity and systematic fixed income businesses have grown assets under management to more than $150bn.

Tech
Broadcom Bid Marks Upheaval in Chip Industry; The chip maker made an unsolicited $105 billion takeover bid for Qualcomm

By Ted Greenwald 
1,109 words
6 November 2017
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

Broadcom was formed when Avago Technologies Ltd. bought the former Broadcom in 2015 for $37 billion and kept the name. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated the purchase price was $39 billion. (Nov. 7, 2017)

Broadcom Ltd. made an unsolicited $105 billion takeover bid for Qualcomm Inc., the chip industry's boldest bet yet that size will equal strength at a time of technological upheaval.

The approach, which would mark the biggest technology takeover ever, shows how tech companies are positioning themselves for a world where a range of chip-driven devices from phones to cars to factory robots are transmitting, receiving and processing evermore information.

Broadcom Chief Executive Hock Tan already has used acquisitions to build the company into the fourth-biggest chip maker by market value, part of a wave of industry consolidation as profits on some chips, such as those used in personal computers, are squeezed by sluggish sales and rising costs.

A combination with Qualcomm would create a behemoth whose chips manage communications among consumer devices and appliances, phone service providers, and data centers that are becoming the workhorses in artificial intelligence.

The deal is far from certain. San Diego-based Qualcomm, which said it would consider the proposal, is expected ultimately to rebuff it on the grounds that the price isn't high enough, especially given the significant risk that regulators would block it, according to some analysts. Under typical circumstances, unfriendly bids like this are difficult to pull off; given the sheer size and complexity of Qualcomm, this one could be especially challenging, analysts said Monday.

Broadcom's preference is to strike a friendly deal, but if it fails to do so, it would consider nominating Qualcomm directors who may be more amenable to a transaction, a person familiar with the matter said. The nomination deadline is Dec. 8 and the annual meeting at which the director vote would take place is likely be around March.

Broadcom offered $70 a share for Qualcomm, representing a 28% premium from its closing price on Thursday before news reports on "https://www.wsj.com/articles/broadcom-plans-unsolicited-bid-for-qualcomm-1509733193".

Qualcomm shares ended trading Monday up 1.2% to $62.52, while Broadcom shares were 1.4% higher at $277.52.

Mr. Tan said he has been talking with Qualcomm for over a year about a possible tie-up. "Our strategy has been consistent," Mr. Tan said in an interview. "When a business is No. 1 in technology and No. 1 in market position, we acquire it and put it on our Broadcom platform and grow through that strategy. Qualcomm has a very large sustainable franchise that meets those criteria."

Should the deal be completed, Broadcom would take on Qualcomm's leadership in developing the next wave of cellular technology, known as 5G, which is expected to roll out over the coming two years. That could give Broadcom a new growth engine, as 5G is expected to dramatically accelerate the speed and responsiveness of cellular communications necessary for applications like self-driving cars.

Broadcom was formed when Avago Technologies Ltd. bought the former Broadcom in 2015 for $37 billion and kept the name, and Mr. Tan has continued growing by acquisition. The company sells a diverse line of equipment for networking and communications. Its products include chips for Wi-Fi and Bluetooth technology that connect devices that are closer together technologies that some analysts say are likely to grow less quickly than 5G.

"People will continue to use short-proximity wireless like Wi-Fi and Bluetooth, but the growth and money is clearly in 5G," said analyst Patrick Moorhead of Moor Insights & Strategy.

Overall, Broadcom and Qualcomm have largely complementary product lines. But the possible Broadcom takeover is likely to face intense regulatory scrutiny, given the companies' combined scale and the fact that they are both leaders in Wi-Fi and Bluetooth technology. The companies share customers including Apple Inc., whose iPhones and iPads include components from both Qualcomm and Broadcom.

Qualcomm already has been under pressure from antitrust agencies in several jurisdictions, including the U.S. The company has paid hefty regulatory fines in China, South Korea and Taiwan.

Qualcomm was riding high as recently as a year ago after unveiling the chip industry's largest-ever acquisition: "https://www.wsj.com/articles/qualcomm-to-buy-nxp-semiconductors-1477565063" for NXP Semiconductors NV. The deal hasn't closed yet, and Broadcom said Monday that its proposal would stand regardless of whether Qualcomm's proposed acquisition of NXP is consummated under the current terms.

Since then, a string of hits by regulators, competitors and customers including Apple has left the industry titan in a vulnerable position. Qualcomm's profit in the fiscal year that ended Sept. 24 plummeted 57%, and its share price declined 18% in the 12 months through Thursday's close compared with a 58% rise in the PHLX Semiconductor Sector Index. That was before news of Broadcom's interest sent Qualcomm shares up nearly 13% on Friday.

Funding for the deal would come in the form of loans from a gaggle of banks, with additional cash from Silver Lake Management LLC. The private-equity firm, which already owns a stake in Broadcom, provided a commitment letter for $5 billion in convertible debt. Silver Lake said a substantial portion of that capital would come in the form of an equity investment from its Silver Lake Partners fund, with the remainder from other sources.

The equity contribution would be the single largest in the history of the firm, exceeding the roughly $1 billion it invested in the merger of Dell Inc. and EMC Corp.

Broadcom's bid came days after the Singapore-based company announced plans to relocate its headquarters to the U.S., a move that could make it easier to pursue acquisitions of U.S. targets.

Broadcom's earlier $5.5 billion offer to buy Brocade Communication Systems, based in San Jose, Calif., has been delayed due to a review by the Committee on Foreign Investment in the United States, which reviews international deals that raise concerns about national security.

Any deal to acquire Qualcomm would also receive close scrutiny, experts say. "Anything that has the word semiconductor in it gets rapt attention from CFIUS," said James Lewis of the Center for Strategic and International Studies, a policy think tank. "The move to the U.S. is an effort to tamp down CFIUS concerns."

Dana Cimilluca contributed to this article.

Write to Ted Greenwald at "mailto:Ted.Greenwald@wsj.com"

Heard on the Street
Markets
SoftBank's Blurry Vision; The Japanese tech giant's boss Masayoshi Son likes control. The collapse of the Sprint/T-Mobile deal talks suggests that is slipping from his grasp.

By Jacky Wong 
437 words
6 November 2017
14:07
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

The size of the SoftBank Vision Fund is $93 billion. An earlier version of this story incorrectly said it was a $98 billion fund. (Nov. 6, 2017)

For Masayoshi Son, control is all important. The question right now is whether too much is slipping from the SoftBank chief executive's grasp.

Mr. Son spent much of SoftBank's latest earnings call Monday explaining why its 83%-owned U.S. telecom operator Sprint has abandoned merger talks with T-Mobile. When U.S. regulators opposed a previous attempt at the deal in 2014, Mr. Son complained he had lost hair as a result.

This time around, Mr. Son pronounced himself happy "https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-inside-story-of-how-the-sprint-and-t-mobile-deal-collapsed-again-1509838624", arguing it was more important for SoftBank to retain control of the company: A deal with the larger T-Mobile now would likely see Sprint as the junior partner. Instead, SoftBank is actually raising its stake in Sprint further, to 85%.

The decision, however, means Sprint will have to carry on fighting alone against its "https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-a-t-mobile-sprint-merger-would-have-changed-the-u-s-market-1509829023" including T-Mobile while having to stretch its "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-ought-to-back-a-sprint-and-t-mobile-marriage-1509425094" to fund billions of dollars of new investment in next-generation networks.

Sprint isn't the only problem for Mr. Son. The $93 billion "https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-worlds-largest-tech-fund-can-set-softbank-stock-free-1489056061" that SoftBank launched in May along with the Saudi Arabian government reported decent results, with an operating profit of $1.6 billion so far. Yet much of that came from only one investment the fund's 4.9% stake in chip designer Nvidia. Shares in the company, which designs graphic chips used in artificial intelligence, have doubled this year.

The past weekend's "https://www.wsj.com/articles/saudi-crackdown-doesnt-guarantee-aramco-ipo-or-higher-oil-1509953962" may not have too much bearing on the Vision Fund. What does seem strange is that one of the world's biggest technology fund's latest deals is to buy a significant minority stake in Saudi Electricity Company. It's also planning to build the world's biggest solar power plant in a new city in Saudi Arabia called "https://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2017/10/26/urban-myth-or-hot-ipo-candidate/".

But how are such investments consistent with SoftBank's aim of leading the "information revolution?" Mr. Son's answer: Saudi Arabia "has great sunshine" and that "without electricity, you can't use phones." That's a practical enough response, but hardly one that burnishes his credentials as a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbanks-vision-fund-will-keep-tech-unicorns-happy-1490700816". Mr. Son will need to do more to stop any suspicion growing that he has bitten off more than he can chew.

As society faces the threats posed by aging infrastructure, one private-equity firm is backing a business that assists in maintaining an essential element of that infrastructure: the pipes that carry our water and gas.

NewWorld Capital Group and a Credit Suisse Group-advised fund invested $26 million in Aquam Corp., a provider of risk-mitigation technologies for water and energy transmission and distribution assets.

The San Diego company makes technologies that aid operators of water and energy infrastructure in assessing and resolving issues in their pipeline systems, including pipe diagnostics, pipe condition assessment and analytics; pipe rehabilitation; and remote water metering and data management. The company has more than 250 employees and 20 locations in the U.S., Canada, the U.K. and Ireland, and serves the utility, commercial and residential property owner markets.

The new capital will be used for product development and the geographic expansion of Aquam's business units, according to a press release. These include Nu Flow Technologies, which makes small-diameter infrastructure lining technologies; pipe lining installer Specialized Pipe Technologies; Aquam Water Services, a U.K. provider of water usage data to utilities; and Aquam Pipe Diagnostics, which specializes in pipeline assessment and inspection.

NewWorld Capital, of New York, backs renewable power and infrastructure projects, typically investing between $10 million and $50 million. Sectors of focus include energy efficiency; water reclamation and re-use; clean energy; environmental products and services; and food and agriculture. Portfolio companies include solar energy provider PosiGen Inc., and Soltage LLC, a developer of solar power stations.

Business
SoftBank Chairman Changes Tune on Sprint's Need for Consolidation; Masayoshi Son defends ending talks with T-Mobile, saying Sprint will blossom as more devices connect to networks

By Mayumi Negishi and Phred Dvorak 
726 words
6 November 2017
12:14
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

TOKYO The head of Sprint Corp.'s parent defended his decision to end merger talks with T-Mobile US Inc., saying Sprint's business is likely to blossom over the next decade as more devices connect to networks.

"I'm glad we were not rushed into a decision that would have hurt the company over the long term," said Masayoshi Son, chairman of Japanese technology giant SoftBank Group Corp.

Shares in SoftBank fell 2.6% in Tokyo on Monday after news that "https://www.wsj.com/articles/sprint-and-t-mobile-calling-off-merger-1509818355" the second time in recent years that a push for a deal collapsed just short of the finish line. Still, the shares are up 28% this year.

After the market close, SoftBank reported an 81% drop in quarterly earnings, a result it attributed to losses on hedging transactions that it expects to be reversed later.

Mr. Son had approached T-Mobile US's parent, Deutsche Telekom AG, about a merger between the two carriers even before SoftBank took control of Sprint for $22 billion in 2013. Since then, he has argued repeatedly that only by consolidating would Sprint have the scale to challenge bigger rivals Verizon Communications Inc. and AT&T Inc.

At a news conference Monday in Tokyo, Mr. Son said he had sought a way to retain control of the merged entity. When it became clear that was impossible, he said he urged his board to drop the deal, since he viewed Sprint as a critical player for the time when a vastly increased number of devices would be communicating over a network.

"Things may be tough for three to four years," Mr. Son said. But "Sprint will become critical for SoftBank as a group in five years' to 10 years' time."

He said, without giving details, that he was still looking for potential partners for Sprint.

Mr. Son, known for successful bets on China's Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. and Yahoo, has been stepping up deal-making in areas where he sees radical change coming, such as robotics, artificial intelligence and auto technologies. In May, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-saudis-to-launch-100-billion-tech-fund-1495270854".

It has poured money into companies like office-sharing firm WeWork Cos., robotics startup Boston Dynamics and Indian e-commerce company Flipkart Group, and it is pursuing a multibillion-dollar investment in ride-hailing company Uber Technologies Inc. Although most of SoftBank's revenue still comes from Sprint and a mobile-phone provider in Japan, Mr. Son said his focus is on deal-making.

"I've never thought SoftBank's main business was telecommunications not for one day, not for one second," he said, adding that the company will invest "in the majority of the world's unicorns" that is, startups valued at more than $1 billion.

Mr. Son reaffirmed that SoftBank is negotiating for a stake in Uber but said he wouldn't go forward if the terms aren't right. "It's still very possible for us to shift our investment target to Lyft," he said, referring to Uber's main U.S. rival.

SoftBank's results for the July-September quarter, released Monday, underscored the impact of the investment push. The company booked an accounting loss of 247.6 billion ($2.2 billion) connected to derivatives transactions it entered into last year, when it started to sell Alibaba shares to raise money to buy U.K. chip-design company ARM Holdings PLC.

The derivatives limit SoftBank's gains and losses from the share sale. But they also require the company to book a temporary paper loss if Alibaba shares rise, as they have. Mr. Son said the losses would be completely reversed in 2019 when the derivatives are unwound.

Operating profit rose 20% to 395.6 billion thanks to the Vision Fund's investment in chip maker Nvidia, whose shares have appreciated. Without the Vision Fund, which includes investments from a Saudi sovereign-wealth fund and tech giants such as Apple Inc., operating profit would have fallen 4%, SoftBank said.

Write to Mayumi Negishi at "mailto:mayumi.negishi@wsj.com" and Phred Dvorak at "mailto:phred.dvorak@wsj.com"
