Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Malaysia Airlines loses another boss as woes deepen

CK Tan, Nikkei staff writer 
1,631 words
30 October 2017
02:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The shock resignation of another chief executive has thrust Malaysia Airlines back into the spotlight, its ongoing troubles highlighting the difficulty of reforming and reviving state-owned companies. With a general election looming, the question is whether political expediency will trump business sense as sovereign fund Khazanah Nasional, the airline s owner, deals with the crisis.

Khazanah s management board is chaired by Prime Minister Najib Razak, who is himself embroiled in a scandal over the alleged mismanagement of billions of dollars at state fund 1Malaysia Development Berhad. On October 14, thousands of people gathered on the outskirts of Kuala Lumpur demanding action against Mr Najib over the 1MDB affair.

Khazanah, which embarked on a five-year restructuring of Malaysia Airlines in 2014, is now scrambling to contain the latest crisis, triggered by the resignation of chief executive Peter Bellew. It came to light on October 17 that Mr Bellew was leaving the company to rejoin budget airline Ryanair as chief operations officer in Ireland, his home country.

Three days later, the fund approved his replacement, choosing Izham Ismail, the carrier s chief operating officer and a former pilot,  in line with [a] succession plan  outlined under the airline s turnround plan.

 I am confident Izham will resume the turnround efforts to make Malaysia Airlines competitive, strong and profitable,  Mr Najib said in a recent Twitter post.

As chief operating officer, Mr Izham was responsible for flight and airport operations as well as engineering.

Mr Bellew took over after his predecessor, Christoph Mueller, also resigned abruptly in April 2016. But Mr Bellew s departure is hitting harder. Although he tendered his resignation on October 8, Malaysia Airlines only released a statement on the matter after Ryanair told the London Stock Exchange about Mr Bellew s appointment in a filing on October 17.

Mr Bellew had been on administrative leave since tendering his resignation and is due to take up his new post on December 1.

The same day, an article in Malaysia s state-controlled New Straits Times written by the paper s chief executive Jalil Hamid said Mr Bellew, like Mr Mueller, had disagreed with some decisions made by Khazanah over how to run the carrier. Nine of the 16 board members at Malaysia Airlines are from Khazanah and other government agencies.

Khazanah responded with a statement slamming the article as  erroneous and misleading . It denied it was  micromanaging  the airline.  The restructuring of Malaysia Airlines follows a well laid-out governance and approval framework,  it said. The statement did not address Mr Bellew s resignation directly.

Mr Bellew also denied that Khazanah had interfered in his job.  People always ask me how do I cope with government interference. There has been none for me,  Mr Bellew said in an email to the Nikkei Asian Review on October 18.  Although possibly I drove Khazanah mad with my constant questions and wish for consensus,  he added.

An aviation expert with more than two decades of experience, Mr Bellew said he viewed the offer from Ryanair as a  form of national service , one that he felt he could not refuse. Dublin-based Ryanair, Europe s largest airline by number of passengers, has been caught up in its own set of problems after it cancelled thousands of flights scheduled for this winter due to what it said was a problem in the scheduling of its pilots. Michael O Leary, chief executive, said Mr Bellew would be responsible for improving working conditions for pilots as part of a  significant transformation. 

In a statement on October 22, Mr Bellew apologised for walking away from his contract, citing family reasons as another factor in his decision.

For Khazanah, the departures of Mr Bellew and Mr Mueller are an embarrassment, given that they were  globally sourced  to turn the carrier round. Both had signed three-year contracts to help Malaysia Airlines return to profitability by the end of 2019 and subsequently refloat its shares.

Khazanah did not mention any of Mr Bellew s contributions in announcing the new Malaysia Airlines chief executive. In a similar announcement last year, it thanked Mr Mueller for his  instrumental contribution  to the restructuring efforts.

Khazanah took the carrier private at the end of 2014 in the aftermath of two disasters that hammered the airline s shares and reputation. Flight 370 disappeared en route from Kuala Lumpur to Beijing in March 2014 and is yet to be found, and Flight 17 was shot down over Ukraine in July that year.

But Malaysia Airlines was struggling with high costs and making losses even before the twin tragedies. In the 2014 recovery plan, Khazanah said the airline s operational costs were much higher than those of its peers and instructed the new management under Mr Mueller to cut 6,000 staff from the total workforce of 20,000. Recently, Mr Bellew said the airline was on the brink of profitability.

A bloated workforce is one of the most difficult inefficiencies to tackle at state-controlled enterprises, which have the added social responsibility of providing jobs. Managing such companies   whose employees tend to form the bedrock of support for the ruling government   can be a tricky task, even for industry veterans such as Mr Mueller and Mr Bellew, who once called it the  toughest job in aviation .

But tough is exactly what the next boss of Malaysia Airlines must be. Aviation analysts say that in addition to expert management, the company needs freedom from government influence.

 The airline is beyond rehabilitation and saving until and unless certain hard-nosed decisions are made,  said Shukor Yusof, an industry veteran who runs his own consulting company Endau Analytics.  That means the immediate removal of those who are incompetent and inept. 

Malaysia Airlines is also fighting a reputation for substandard service. Its Facebook page is regularly flooded with comments complaining of poor customer service, flight cancellations and its unwieldy mobile site.

The repeated turnover at the top   three chief executives in three years   makes the already challenging turnround even more difficult to achieve, said Brendan Sobie, an analyst at the Sydney-based Centre for Aviation.

On the other hand, one local analyst said the carrier was showing signs of progress in its restructuring thanks to the two foreign executives doing the  dirty work  of getting rid of inefficiencies.

The government, Mr Shukor said, is answerable to taxpayers for the  deep rut  the carrier is in despite having spent $1.42bn to bail it out.

The trouble at Malaysia Airlines stands out all the more when compared with compatriot AirAsia. The budget airline founded by entrepreneur Tony Fernandes carried 15.8m passengers in the second quarter of this year, compared with Malaysia Airlines  3.6m. AirAsia s load factor, a key indicator of airline performance, stood at 88 per cent for the quarter versus 77. 8 per cent for its state-owned counterpart.

With the country due to go to the polls by August 2018, government interference in state companies such as Malaysia Airlines is unlikely to let up.

In one recent example, Mr Najib stepped in to sort out a boardroom tussle at Felda Global Ventures, removing the chairman of the palm plantation company for alleged corruption.

FGV is controlled by the Federal Land Development Authority, which provides support for more than 100,000 farming households through handouts in the form of subsidised fertiliser and low-interest loans. This support has helped ensure the ruling party s popularity in rural constituencies.

In at least one case, however, desperation has led to a more hands-off management approach. Earlier this year, the government approved the sale of a 49.9 per cent stake in Proton Holdings to China s Zhejiang Geely Holding Group to bail out the lossmaking national carmaker. It also had no objection to the appointment of Chinese national Li Chunrong as Proton s new chief and said the partnership with Geely was a  commercial decision  on the part of the seller, DRB-Hicom.

Khazanah owned a majority stake in Proton before divesting it in 2012 to DRB-Hicom in hopes that the local automobile conglomerate could turn it round.

Lacking economies of scale, Proton has for years suffered from the widespread perception that its vehicles are of poor quality. Sales have steadily declined, and today the company controls only about 14 per cent of the domestic market, even though its cars are priced lower than its competitors thanks to preferential treatment.

In the case of Malaysia Airlines, the stakes are even higher. Mr Najib promised US president Donald Trump in a visit to Washington in September that Malaysia would step up investment in America. Mr Bellew then announced during the trip that Malaysia Airlines would buy eight wide-body 787 Dreamliners made by Boeing of the US.

Former Malaysian prime minister Mahathir Mohamad said Mr Bellew s departure was caused by Mr Najib s instructing the company to place the aircraft order to please Mr Trump, an allegation that Mr Bellew said is  100 per cent incorrect .

How Khazanah handles the awkwardness created by Mr Bellew s sudden exit will have an impact not only the airline itself. After all, as Mr Najib said in his Twitter post, Malaysia Airlines is not just any carrier. It carries the name, image and brand of the country in the skies.  It is our pride. 

A version of this article was first published by the Nikkei Asian Review on October 26. 2017 Nikkei Inc. All rights reserved.

Pro Private Markets
Private Equity Analyst's 2017 Women to Watch: Venture Capital

By Alexander Davis, Brian Gormley, Cat Zakrzewski, Heather Mack, Yuliya Chernova and Tomio Geron 
2,367 words
30 October 2017
13:34
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

This year has been marked by soul searching about sexism in the venture-capital industry. Waves of headlines about sexual misconduct among venture capitalists have only underscored the industry's shortcomings in diversity and shed light on the importance of having strong women in influential leadership positions.

The good news is that a high-achieving cadre of women has answered the call over the years and continues to make strides as investors at firms large and small, old and new.

In the latest installment of our second Women to Watch series, Private Equity Analyst profiles eight female influencers in the industry.

KATE CASTLE

Marketing Partner, Flybridge Capital Partners Operating Partner, XFactor Ventures

In her junior year at Wheaton College, Kate Castle spent a semester in Indonesia, where she linked up with a nonprofit that made microloans to women looking to start their own businesses. That project became a passion and foreshadowed her current role with XFactor Ventures, a $3 million fund unveiled in July with plans to back 30 startups with female founders. In the process, the firm hopes to draw more women into a startup ecosystem currently dominated by men. "I have an attitude [that] there's always a fix for something," Ms. Castle said. She began her career in public relations before taking marketing roles with Battery Ventures and IDG Ventures Boston, which rebranded as Flybridge Capital Partners in 2008. Today she is marketing partner of Flybridge and co-founder of XFactor along with Flybridge's Chip Hazard and Anna Palmer, co-founder and chief executive officer of startup Wondermile. XFactor, whose investment partners include eight female startup founders, targets pain points facing women on the fundraising trail. Securing a first check is a tough task, and slow investment decisions make it even tougher. XFactor makes decisions within three weeks. As an XFactor operating partner, a position similar to her role with Flybridge, Ms. Castle filters entrepreneurs to the appropriate investment partner, connects companies with resources and handles the firm's marketing. XFactor's efforts could bring more diversity to startups, but a variety of solutions are needed to solve the problem of too few women receiving venture funding, according to Ms. Castle, 46. "There isn't a silver bullet for how we do this," she said.

KIRSTEN GREEN

General Partner, Forerunner Ventures

It doesn't take more than a glance at the team roster of Forerunner Ventures to see what makes the San Francisco firm different from other venture-capital firms: Almost all of the partners are women. "Given all the conversations about the problems in the industry, I'm really proud of the diversity of our team," said Kirsten Green, 45, who added the investing partners also range three decades in age and hail from different parts of the country. "Those varied perspectives are what give us vision and an exemplified business lens." That foundational belief is what helped Forerunner, which Ms. Green founded in 2003, to scout some of the most successful retail startups of the past few years. The firm was an early backer of businesses that yielded some of the largest exits in the realm of online retail in the last 18 months. Jet.com Inc. was acquired by Wal-Mart Stores Inc. for about $3.3 billion, and Unilever bought Dollar Shave Club Inc. for $1 billion. Ms. Green also occupies several board seats at startups including beauty-product company Glossier Inc. and the recently acquired online men's clothier Bonobos Inc. She attributes her track record with early-stage companies that strike a chord with consumers to her own experience watching the evolution of commerce. Previously, she worked at places including private-equity firm TSG Consumer Partners, Banc of America Securities LLC and Deloitte, before the burgeoning online shopping ecosystem drew her into the venture world.

PAULINA HILL

Principal, Polaris Partners

Paulina Hill majored in four subjects at East Carolina University while also captaining the women's tennis team a fitting preparation for her work at Polaris Partners, where she backs a diverse group of biomedical startups. As a principal in the Boston office of Polaris, Dr. Hill works with drugmakers such as Kala Pharmaceuticals Inc., which went public in July, and medical-technology businesses such as Neuronetics Inc., and last year helped launch Marauder Therapeutics, a biotechnology company the firm seeded. An investment career wasn't what she had in mind while studying biology, biochemistry, chemistry and neuroscience at East Carolina, nor while she was earning a Ph.D. in molecular medicine at Wake Forest University School of Medicine. But she did know she wanted to transform academic discoveries into disease treatments a desire that led her, after a postdoctoral fellowship at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, to join Polaris in 2012. Polaris, which launches and invests in startups, affords her the opportunity to employ her scientific expertise as she evaluates medical discoveries and helps create companies to develop them. Marauder uses artificial intelligence and the new science of gene control to identify novel drug targets and therapeutics. Dr. Hill, 35, until recently was Marauder's CEO. Now she is exploring new prospects in fields of interest to her, including women's health, where she sees significant medical needs and improving innovation. "I want to work with smart people on cool things," she said. "And I get to do that every day."

SHADI MEHRAEIN

General Partner, Rivet Ventures

Shadi Mehraein has an extensive background in venture investing. Now she is building her own firm. Rivet Ventures targets women-focused markets and consumers, an area often overlooked in the male-dominated venture world. It is investing out of its first $15 million fund. San Francisco-based Rivet is one of a handful of venture firms started in recent years by female investors. Ms. Mehraein, 34, is a general partner at Rivet along with Rebeca Hwang, who was co-founder of startup YouNoodle Inc. Two other partners are Christina Brodbeck, a founding designer at YouTube and angel investor; and Stephanie Tilenius, a former Google executive who has done an executive-in-residence stint at Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers. Previously, Ms. Mehraein was with growth-stage firm Focus Ventures, where she worked on investments in startups including BookRenter, Delivery Agent, Marin Software Inc. and PCH International. Before that, she was at Bessemer Venture Partners, working on areas such as e-commerce, software as a service, gaming and digital media. Rivet focuses on female consumers and markets, though it invests in male and female founders, and also could target deals in sectors such as health, education and social media. Ms. Mehraein has seen firsthand the challenges facing female founders. "It is fairly safe to say that both female entrepreneurs and investors are often held to a different, if not higher, standard when fundraising," she said. One way to change that, she said, is to have more women as venture investors, adding, "We would like to see more money go to female GPs because it all trickles down."

RENATA QUINTINI

Partner, Lux Capital

Last year wasn't too shabby for Renata Quintini. Two of her portfolio companies, self-driving car technology developer Cruise Automation Inc. and e-commerce startup Dollar Shave Club, were picked up in billion-dollar acquisitions by strategic buyers. In February 2017, Ms. Quintini left Felicis Ventures, where she was general partner, to become a partner at Lux Capital to focus exclusively on science-based technology investments. "It's a pure play," Ms. Quintini said about Lux and why she decided to join the firm. "I wanted to spend 100% of my time helping build the cutting edge." At Lux, Ms. Quintini, 38, is focusing on early-stage startups solving problems in food, whether it is materials that help store food longer or vertical farming that gets healthier food to consumers. She is looking at technology for older people, who, Ms. Quintini said, are an expanding demographic that deserves as much attention as millennials from the technology sector. She also is paying attention to fertility. Robotics and automation remain interests as well. At Lux, Ms. Quintini has made one investment, backing Common Networks Inc., a provider of wireless networks for suburban communities. It is a complicated business, Ms. Quintini said, that involves the development of software and hardware, as well as the logistics of rolling out a truck to people's homes. "It's a multidisciplinary problem," and that, she said, is the type of business that excites her. Ms. Quintini first became aware of venture capital as a lawyer in Brazil, where some of her clients were venture investors, she said. She came to the U.S. and received a master's degree in business administration at Stanford University. That led her to work at the Stanford University endowment, where she interacted with many venture investors, including a wave of micro-venture-capital funds, which, in turn, led Ms. Quintini to join Felicis. She said that increasing the number of diverse voices in venture capital and startups is crucial. "Tech has a little bit of an empathy problem," Ms. Quintini said, adding that some startup investments are out of touch with the problems many people face or sometimes, even, may hurt people. "Cognitive diversity" would help technologists avoid problems and understand society better, she said.

KRISTINA SHEN

Partner, Bessemer Venture Partners

Kristina Shen says she isn't much into self-promotion. But her Bessemer Venture Partners colleagues who pushed her to take more risks in boosting her brand seem to have proven she is up to the challenge. Her feet may be on the ground, but her head is very much in the cloud. Learning to overcome her reticence and aversion to public speaking, she now is considered to be one of the venture-capital industry's foremost experts on the cloud computing market, along with her Bessemer mentor Byron Deeter. And her rise to the forefront didn't take long. Ms. Shen graduated college in 2009, then worked in investment banking before making the leap into venture capital. She worked at JMI Equity before joining Bessemer in 2013. "I'm a data nerd," she said. "Numbers make sense to me." Together with Mr. Deeter, Ms. Shen is the author of Bessemer's annual "State of the Cloud," an authoritative look at the top trends in the market and a scoreboard about who is up and who is down in what has become a dominant area of business for today's venture-backed companies. Ms. Shen, 30, has helped lead Bessemer investments into startups using cloud-based platforms that cover a variety of business productivity and marketing segments. She is involved with the boards of businesses including Buildscale Inc., a video provider for marketers that does business as Vidyard; ServiceTitan Inc., which is used by service technicians and other contractors in the field; and, her proudest investment, Glint Inc., a human-resources tool for employee engagement. Ms. Shen helped lead a $37 Series C round in Glint last year. New pressure on the industry to combat sexism and boost diversity is welcome news to Ms. Shen. She said venture capitalists must work on all levels to counter unconscious bias, adding investors too often inadequately evaluate founders because they fall back on superficial pattern recognition. "I'm glad it's a hot topic," she said. "It's getting much-needed attention."

LISA SUENNEN

Senior Managing Director, GE Ventures

Privately held health-care companies may have slightly better representation of women leaders than do businesses in the technology space or other areas of investment, but Lisa Suennen of GE Ventures says she sees more than enough room for improvement, and she was determined to do something about it. To encourage more women to enter executive roles, Ms. Suennen last year co-founded a not-for-profit program called CSweetener. The group pairs rising professional women with established mentors both female and male alike. Ms. Suennen, senior managing director of health-care investing at GE Ventures, said since its inception, CSweetener has drawn hundreds of participants, both mentors and mentees. "I had a responsibility to do what I could do. I appreciate that people do things like researching the problem and publishing reports and making statements, but it's much more interesting to take action," said Ms. Suennen, 51. "I had a platform to be able to act." In addition to working on leadership issues, she is a well-known voice inside the health-care industry. Ms. Suennen focuses on the intersection of health-care information technology and services and sits on the boards of several companies, including Evidation Health Inc. and Gravie Inc. On top of all that, Ms. Suennen is managing partner of media publishing business Venture Valkyrie LLC, where she writes a popular blog and co-produces a podcast called Tech Tonics. Previously, Ms. Suennen spent about 15 years at health care-focused firm Psilos Group Managers. She also lectures on health-care investing and the evolving health-care economy at the University of California, Berkeley's Haas School of Business.

SARAH TAVEL

General Partner, Benchmark

As Benchmark's newest partner, Sarah Tavel has met with a few hundred entrepreneurs since joining the firm in May. A former investor at Greylock Partners and former Pinterest Inc. product lead, Ms. Tavel is known for her focus on consumer technology. But since joining Benchmark, Ms. Tavel, 35, has been spending more time on cryptocurrency startups. Though she hasn't yet announced any investments in her new role, Ms. Tavel is bullish on bitcoin's potential. She does warn that the crypto industry is in a bubble when it comes to its frenzy of fundraising via initial coin offerings. Since joining Benchmark, Ms. Tavel has joined HMFDF Inc., which does business as Solv Health, as a board observer and also has visited with other Benchmark portfolio companies, such as Glassdoor Inc. and HackerOne. "Every meeting, I walk away with an insight or a way of thinking about our business that we didn't have before," Heather Fernandez, Solv's CEO, said of Ms. Tavel.

The move adds an automobile industry professional to TPG's industrials practice as it seeks to expand supply chain-related investments. The firm said it wants to tap Mr. Fields's expertise as it looks for opportunities to create value at industrial companies by harnessing new developments in online commerce, outsourcing and big data.

Arsenal Capital Partners named Patricia Grad as a senior managing director and head of investor relations. Before joining the midmarket firm, Ms. Grad was a director in the investor development group at Actis.

Leah Busque, co-founder of freelance labor marketplace TaskRabbit Inc., joined early-stage investor Fuel Capital as its second general partner. Ms. Busque stepped down as CEO of TaskRabbit last year and became the company's executive chairwoman.

General Catalyst named Holly Maloney McConnell as a managing director in Boston focused on later-stage investments. She previously was a principal at Guidepost Growth Equity, formerly known as North Bridge Growth Equity, where she primarily made software-related investments.

Ant Puts Plans for An IPO on Hold

By Chuin-Wei Yap 
525 words
28 October 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B11
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

HONG KONG -- Flush with cash, Chinese financial-technology firm Ant Financial Services Group is putting on hold plans for an initial public offering while it steps up investments in everything from startups to artificial intelligence, according to a senior company executive.

Ant, which was founded by billionaire Jack Ma and born out of a spinoff from Alibaba Group Holding Ltd., has quietly grown into one of the world's most valuable fintech companies. Its biggest asset is Alipay, a mobile-payments platform used on Alibaba's e-commerce sites and by hundreds of millions of Chinese consumers to pay for everything from meals to college tuition fees.

Investors and analysts have been expecting Ant to go public sometime in 2018. The company last raised $4.5 billion from private investors in April 2016 in a deal that gave it a $60 billion valuation -- and its business has since expanded significantly.

An IPO "is not a necessity or a priority" right now, said Douglas Feagin, Ant's president of international business, in an interview with The Wall Street Journal in Hong Kong.

Mr. Feagin, an American who was a Goldman Sachs Group Inc. investment banker for more than two decades, said he can appreciate the benefits of going public but Ant has other, more pressing, plans to implement.

Among Ant's pursuits: expanding Alipay's global footprint within and beyond the 70 countries the payments system is used in, while plumbing for new technology investments globally in things like facial-recognition and machine-learning systems.

"It is the time now to deploy artificial intelligence in ways that are much more sophisticated than we've ever done: The technology is there, the data is there, [as is] the receptivity of consumers," Mr. Feagin said.

He declined to detail exactly how much Ant plans to invest, but pointed to affiliate Alibaba's plans to spend more than $15 billion over three years on research and development.

Ant has, in the past three years, invested more than $16 billion in more than 50 startups that include online lenders, insurers and technology firms, according to database ITjuzi.

The company led a $40 million financing round announced Tuesday for DeePhi Tech, a Beijing-based startup that creates applications for "deep learning," a technology that teaches computers to differentiate data the way a human brain does.

As a closely held business, Ant doesn't disclose its financial results publicly. But data from Alibaba -- to which Ant pays fixed royalties and fees under a profit-sharing agreement -- indicate Ant's pretax profit reached $773 million in the quarter ended in June. That is close to Ant's profit for its entire previous fiscal year ended in March, which rose 86% from a year earlier to $814 million, according to Alibaba filings.

Some analysts expect the company to go public eventually. "Ant Financial's strategy is to defend share, grow into new areas and generate profits -- leading to the business becoming 'IPO-ready' in 2018," said Sanford C. Bernstein & Co. research analyst Bhavtosh Vajpayee.

U.S. Economy Notches Solid 3% Growth, Despite Hurricanes; GDP growth remained strong in the face of storms that temporarily shuttered major population centers in Texas and Florida

By Josh Mitchell 
1,125 words
27 October 2017
22:42
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Corrections & Amplifications

U.S. GDP, when adjusted for inflation and seasonality, rose 2.3% from a year earlier in the third quarter of 2017. A line on a chart in a previous version of this article misstated the GDP-growth level by about 2 percentage points between 1990 and 2017. (Oct. 28)

WASHINGTON The U.S. economy posted its best six-month stretch of growth in three years despite two hurricanes, a sign that it might be breaking out of its long-running slow-growth trend, with the help of soaring stock prices and rising business and consumer confidence.

Gross domestic product, the broadest measure of goods and services produced in the U.S., expanded at a 3% annual rate in the third quarter, the Commerce Department said Friday. That followed 3.1% annual growth in the spring.

Though not a boom, that's still the first time since mid-2014 that the economy has strung together two quarters of at least 3% growth. Solid spending by consumers and businesses along with higher sales of American goods overseas helped prevent the kind of slowdown some economists expected after hurricanes Harvey and Irma shut down parts of Texas and Florida in August and September.

The third-quarter gain came alongside signals that "animal spirits," as the economist John Maynard Keynes once described the desire to spend and invest, were helping to boost economic activity. Stock-market gains have accelerated since Donald Trump was elected president last year, and both consumer and small-business confidence have notched higher. The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose another 33 points Friday, putting it up 18.4% so far this year.

In the wake of those sentiment advances, household purchases of durable goods, such as cars and refrigerators, have picked up, as have gains in business investment in equipment. Consumer spending on durable goods rose at an 8.3% annual rate in the third quarter after 7.6% annual growth in the previous quarter. Equipment spending by business rose at an annual 8.6%, following 8.8% in the previous quarter.

Kevin Wilson, chief executive and president of Buzz Franchise Brands, a pest-control and home-cleaning company, said higher stocks have led several people to open franchises under his brands this year.

"Good times are here," he said. "Their stocks have gone up and they're taking some of that money and saying now's the opportunity to start our own business."

Still, there is ample reason for caution before concluding the gains will be sustained. The economy has produced six-month growth bursts several times during this eight-year expansion, including a period of near-5% growth in mid-2014. But it hasn't been able to keep up that pace. The growth rate as a whole for the expansion has averaged 2.2%.

A chunk of the third quarter's growth reflected businesses' replenishing of stockpiles, which had fallen earlier this year, rather than higher sales. Stripping out inventory changes, which can be volatile, output grew at a 2.3% rate, closer to the longer-running average. Compared with a year earlier, overall output including inventory changes was just 2.3% higher.

While economists are expecting growth in the current quarter to clock near 3% again, nagging trends, including an aging population and weak worker productivity gains, still might stand in the way of the country achieving sustained robust economic growth.

"We have to be a little bit careful," said Gregory Daco, economist at Oxford Economics. "The underlying pace still remains in that 2% growth environment."

President Donald Trump has promised sustained 3% growth by reducing regulations, revamping trade agreements and passing a broad tax-cut package currently being negotiated in Congress.

Administration officials celebrated the latest numbers. "As the President's tax-cut plan is implemented, our entire economy will continue to come roaring back," Commerce Secretary Wilbur Ross said in a statement Friday.

Democrats weren't prepared to yield any credit to the new administration. "Today's GDP numbers show the resiliency of the economy this administration inherited," Sen. Martin Heinrich (D., N.M) said in a statement. "Instead of building on this foundation, President Trump has created a climate of uncertainty."

Hurricanes made it challenging to discern the underlying trend in the third quarter. Damage to property led to a spurt of spending on cars in hurricane-damaged areas, but the storms also appear to have depressed spending on services like restaurants, where payrolls sank for the month.

The Commerce Department said hurricanes likely suppressed business activity such as oil-and-gas extraction in Texas and agricultural production in Florida. But the storms likely boosted other types of activity, such as emergency services and repair efforts.

"It is not possible to estimate the overall impact of Hurricanes Harvey and Irma on 2017 third-quarter GDP," the agency said.

Repair efforts could boost economic growth in coming quarters, particularly in building sectors. The Commerce Department estimated that government and private insurance companies' payouts resulting from the storms could total $102 billion. That's money that could be plowed back into the economy.

Taken altogether, the report is likely to nudge the Federal Reserve closer to raising its benchmark short-term interest rate the federal funds rate at its policy meeting in December. Inflation picked up this summer after weakening earlier in the year, though it remains below the Fed's 2% target. The price index for personal consumption expenditures the Fed's preferred measure of inflation rose at a 1.5% annual rate in the third quarter, up from the second quarter's 0.3% increase.

If the economy is going to sustain faster growth, much will depend on the outlook for business investment, which is central to boosting worker productivity and sustaining robust corporate profit gains.

MBAF, a 600-employee accounting and consulting firm in based in Miami, offered a sign that a sustainable pickup is happening. It has budgeted roughly $1 million in capital spending in its current fiscal year triple the amount it spent last year.

Tony Argiz, the firm's chief executive and chairman, said the spending increase reflects two developments: New technology has come on the market recently enabling his company to perform audits much more efficiently, and breaches at firms like Equifax are pushing firms like his to invest in new security software.

He said the company feels the urge to become more efficient to compete with other firms. "We need to keep up with the times, and artificial intelligence is going to be critical in my profession in the next four or five years," Mr. Argiz said.

Nordea to axe 6,000 jobs in drive to digital future

Richard Milne, Nordic Correspondent 
420 words
26 October 2017
15:32
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Nordea is cutting around 6,000 jobs as the Nordic region s biggest bank battles to maintain its competitiveness in the shift towards more digital services.

The bank, which will move its headquarters from Sweden to Finland next year, will axe about 4,000 of its 32,000 workers plus at least 2,000 consultants.

Nordea will take a charge of  ¬100m to  ¬150m in the fourth quarter as well as more costs over the next four years for what it calls its  transformation .

It is part of a shift towards digital services that has seen the bank close branches and employ a chatbot using artificial intelligence to answer common customer queries.

Casper von Koskull, chief executive, said on Thursday that Nordea needed to  structurally bring down costs and increase efficiency .

Analysts expressed surprise at how high costs would remain despite the job cuts. Mr von Koskull said total costs would be  ¬4.9bn next year and would only fall below  ¬4.8bn by 2021.

Unions reacted strongly to the job losses.  It is absolutely incomprehensible that you cut every eighth job at the same time as presenting a net profit for the first nine months of  ¬2.4bn,  said Kent Pedersen, head of the financial services union in Denmark.

He added that Nordea was following a  very strict focus on shareholders  at the expense of workers.

Investors begged to differ, sending Nordea s shares down by 6 per cent to SKr101.4.

The bank has invested heavily in digital services in what it portrays as an attempt to focus more on customer service. It started deploying a customer service chatbot in Norway that has so far answered about 10,000 customer questions. In December, it will launch an online robot adviser that can give investments and savings advice.

The news on job cuts comes amid a big upheaval at the bank, which has risen to become one of the most valuable banks in Europe after relatively successfully weathering the 2008 financial crisis.

It plans to move its headquarters and a handful of jobs from Stockholm to Helsinki next year in part to escape  ¬1bn in regulatory fees. But some investors think that by joining the eurozone and its banking union it may be looking for takeover deals in the future.

Nordea also announced third-quarter results that were largely in line with analysts  expectations. Operating profit was  ¬1.09bn versus last year s  ¬1.15bn.

Opinion
Regulators Can Help American Workers Get the Credit They Deserve; Nonprime lending isn't as risky as it's made out to be, and fintech firms can help traditional banks.

By William M. Isaac and Ken Rees 
866 words
27 October 2017
00:05
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

This month the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau issued new rules likely to curtail payday and title loans. On the same day, the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency removed its ban on short-term subprime lending by banks. Now some banks are sorting through ways to help meet demand for these loans.

These changes come amid turbulent times for millions of Americans. Average savings have dropped to less than 5% of income today from around 13% in the 1970s, according to the "https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/PSAVERT". A 2016 Federal Reserve report estimates nearly half of Americans would not be able to muster $400 for an emergency. Making matters worse, according to the "https://www.brookings.edu/research/the-evolution-of-household-income-volatility-3/", income volatility has risen 30% since 1971. Access to credit has become an essential tool for millions of people dealing with unexpected expenses and income shortfalls.

Banks have also undergone dramatic changes. Automated credit scoring systems have replaced local loan officers, who once made credit decisions based on bank statements, income assessments, community connections and character. These technologies penalize those without pristine credit histories. Regulatory pressures have made the situation worse, forcing banks to curtail lending to the people most in need of it while battling for the relatively small pool of affluent customers.

To regain the trust and business of working-class Americans, bankers and their regulators must encourage innovations that help the underserved. With new leadership coming to the federal banking agencies, we offer five important insights:

 " America is a nonprime nation. An astounding two-thirds of Americans have a nonprime credit score (below 700) or no score at all, according to the Corporation for Enterprise Development and FICO. Due to high credit-score requirements, some 160 million Americans find it difficult or impossible to access traditional bank credit. The way for banks to grow and better serve their communities is to figure out how to lend again, safely and profitably, to a much broader range of customers. Banks need a new generation of nonprime credit products.

 " Nonprime customer needs differ greatly from prime customers. Traditional bank credit products haven't really changed much in recent decades. Banks assume a customer is willing to come to a branch, fill out an application, provide paperwork, and then wait for a decision.

Nonprime customers' need for credit is far more urgent. The top five reasons for selecting a lender are "Solve Financial Need," "Fast and Easy," "Money Quick," "No Hidden Fees" and "Few Documents," according to the Center for the New Middle Class. "Lowest APR" was listed as a top three reason by less than 1 out of 5 non-prime customers.

It is not enough to provide credit for consumers' immediate needs. Banks need to be fully committed to improving the financial wellness of their customers through credit building and personal-finance training.

 " Traditional underwriting approaches don't work. Although FICO scores do a good job of identifying the overall riskiness of customers within different bands of credit scores, they are only minimally predictive for underwriting nonprime consumers. Prime lenders look for customers with pristine credit and typically turn down anyone with a limited credit history or derogatory credit information.

Lending to nonprime customers requires new, targeted credit scores built around specific types of consumers, often using alternative data sources and machine learning techniques. Regulators should support technology-based innovation in underwriting.

 " Nonprime lending can be less risky than prime lending. The real risk in consumer lending stems from changes in the economic environment. Perhaps surprisingly, this is where nonprime lending is actually less volatile. During the Great Recession, charge-off rates for personal loans to prime credit customers increased by a higher percentage than nonprime loans, according to data from the credit reporting firm TransUnion. Changes in the broader business cycle influence prime customers more than nonprime customers, who are used to dealing with financial pressures.

 " Fintech innovators are not the enemy. Most banks outsource the technology used in branch systems and for support of their core products. Fintech innovators should be encouraged to work with banks, not compete against them.

Banks have funding and compliance advantages, and banks already have financial relationships with the majority of Americans. But most banks will have difficulty matching the agility and analytical savvy of the fintech industry. Regulators should be receptive to joint ventures between banks and technology firms.

An outdated regulatory environment, fueled by misperceptions about nonprime lending, has resulted in greater risks for banks and their customers. Loss of market share and profitable sources of income has weakened banks, and consumers are hurt by being driven to more expensive nonbank lenders. The incoming leadership at the federal banking agencies has an opportunity to help banks re-emerge as the lender of choice to tens of millions of working Americans.

Mr. Isaac, a financial consultant, was chairman of the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. (1981-85) and of Fifth Third Bancorp (2010-14). Mr. Rees is CEO of the online lender Elevate.

Business
Ask Me How Much Money I Make: Pay Gets More Transparent; Nearly half of millennials surveyed said they talk about their compensation with friends, compared with 36% of Americans overall

By Kelsey Gee 
610 words
26 October 2017
17:22
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

An earlier version of this article contained a misspelling of Cameron Feenstra's surname in a caption. (Oct. 26, 2017)

Managing a generation of young people inclined to share relationship statuses and meal photos on social media requires employers to adjust the way they approach compensation, experts say.

"Pay and promotions are not secretive topics anymore," says Mary Ann Sardone, who consults with large employers on compensation issues and leads the workforce-rewards practice at benefits consultant Mercer, a unit of Marsh & McLennan Cos.

"Companies are spending more time ensuring their pay decisions are fair, and highlighting career paths under the assumption that the information is going to be widely shared," she says.

Roughly a third of U.S. workers ages 18 to 36 say they feel comfortable discussing pay with their co-workers, more than any other age group and about four times the rate among baby boomers, ages 53 to 71, according to a survey of 1,000 employees conducted by personal finance firm Bankrate Inc.'s TheCashlorette.com. Nearly half of the millennials surveyed say they talk about compensation with their friends, compared with 36% of Americans overall.

When Cameron Feenstra received a job offer this summer from Prattle Analytics, a St. Louis-based research firm, the first thing the 22-year-old did was call his sister. Although he was willing to take a below-market salary for the chance to work at a fast-growing startup, Mr. Feenstra wanted to ensure that his offer of $42,000 was a fair annual salary for his role as a junior quantitative analyst.

After talking about salaries with friends and family, and consulting anonymous career and salary-sharing websites such as Glassdoor, Mr. Feenstra decided to negotiate for more money, even though it was his first real job in the field.

"People who don't ask around never learn how to negotiate, because they don't know where everyone else is" in terms of salary as a reference point, Mr. Feenstra says. He got a pay bump to $45,000 before accepting the offer.

The attitude shift has put greater pressure on employers to explain why some workers are paid more than others and to formalize compensation and promotion practices, says Kristina Launey, a partner at law firm Seyfarth Shaw LLP, which specializes in labor and employment issues.

A "https://www.wsj.com/articles/cities-push-employers-to-ignore-pay-history-1492254003" in Philadelphia, New York City and Massachusetts bar hiring managers from asking job candidates about their salary history, pressuring companies to be more transparent about what they are willing to pay for many roles.

Bill MacMillan, Prattle co-founder and chief technology officer, says he is accustomed to requests like Mr. Feenstra's. But keeping the 19-person startup on good financial footing while offering competitive-enough salaries to retain talented workers is a delicate balance, he adds.

"I have great people, so I would love to pay them lots and lots of money," says Mr. MacMillan. Instead, he says, the firm explains to job seekers that while their salary may start at a below-market level, their performance and pay will typically be reviewed at least twice a year at which point he and other managers can be "aggressive" with raises for top performers.

Since Mr. Feenstra began working at Prattle, he has discussed his pay with several of his colleagues. The chats have given him an idea of what to expect when discussing future raises, such as when his boss reviews his performance later this year.

Write to Kelsey Gee at "mailto:kelsey.gee@wsj.com"

Marlin Equity Partners acquired Logi Analytics Inc., which provides an embedded analytics platform for software-application teams.

Terms of the deal weren't disclosed in a news release.

"Embedded analytics is a digital workplace capability where data analysis occurs within a user's natural workflow and doesn't require toggling to another application," Logi Analytics said.

The McLean, Va., company added it has more than 1,800 customers world-wide.

Marlin, with offices in Hermosa Beach, Calif., and London, invests in software and technology, services, health-care, consumer and industrial companies with annual revenue of $10 million to $2 billion. The firm has more than $6.7 billion of capital under management.

McKinsey predicts $350bn boost for banks from new technology

Martin Arnold 
301 words
25 October 2017
13:09
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Banks could earn an extra $350bn of profit by harnessing the potential of new technology, such as artificial intelligence, big data analytics and cloud computing, according to new research from consultants at McKinsey.

The productivity improvements of digitising their businesses would help banks to partly offset the expected impact of increasing competition from  platform companies  such as Amazon in the US, Rakuten in Japan, or Alibaba and Tencent in China, the research predicted.

 The fintech threat to retail banking might be receding,  McKinsey said.  But the new strategies adopted by the platform companies are even more challenging for incumbent banks. 

 Not only do they have exceptional data that they exploit with remarkable effectiveness, but, more worrisome for banks, they are often more central in the customer journeys that include big financial decisions.

McKinsey s annual review of the banking industry said that if banks took no action the competition from such vast technology-based competitors would cut the industry s return on equity from 8.6 per cent last year to only 5.2 per cent by 2025.

 Our previous research identified two major effects from new digital entrants: the loss of the customer relationship and margin erosion across retail segments,  the report said.  We see new evidence of those trends   and they are happening faster than we expected. Margins continue to fall worldwide. 

McKinsey said the total upside for banks that embrace technology was worth $700bn of extra income by 2025. But it said that some banks were unlikely to make the necessary changes and some of the gains would be  competed away , so it reduced its estimate of the likely gains to $350bn, which would translate into a return on equity of 7.7 per cent by 2025.

The Property Report
Commercial-Property Deals Dry Up

By Esther Fung 
735 words
25 October 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Big U.S. real-estate companies have been selling assets at a slower pace this year, as the gap widens between their views on what their properties are worth and buyers' willingness to pay high prices.

After an eight-year bull run for commercial real estate, some investors have been anticipating a correction. But that hasn't happened yet, and there is little consensus on how much longer the bull market has to run.

Buyers, facing tighter lending conditions and slower income growth, are expecting lower prices and bidding accordingly, but sellers, including publicly traded property owners, are holding out for better deals.

Listed real-estate investment trusts have sold $46.7 billion in assets as of Oct. 23 this year, compared with $71 billion in assets sold in all of 2016, according to data from Real Capital Analytics. Acquisitions, on the other hand, have been at a roughly similar pace at around $44.6 billion as of Oct. 23 this year compared with $47.9 billion in 2016. There have been fewer major transactions especially in the office and retail real-estate sector.

Strong growth in property rents in recent years has fueled deal making, as owners met their targeted yields earlier than expected and buyers were bullish on further appreciation. This year, by contrast, rent growth apart from the industrial sector has been subdued or flat, said Brian McAuliffe, president of institutional properties at real-estate consultancy firm CBRE Group.

Unlike previous cycles, property owners aren't overly leveraged and are still able to access the debt markets rather than be compelled to sell at unattractive prices.

"Sellers are comfortable with the performance of the rental markets if they hold their properties," said Mr. McAuliffe.

Mall REIT GGP Inc. in August said it would continue to lease its malls rather than sell them. That was a turnaround from May, when Chief Executive Sandeep Mathrani said it would explore "strategic alternatives," including a sale of the Chicago-based company following frustration over the public market's low valuation.

"We felt there was a lot of meat on the bone that the board didn't want to leave on the table," said Mr. Mathrani during the second-quarter earnings call in early August. Shares of GGP fell 4.9% that day and have yet to recover to levels in the May to July period.

A dearth in transactions of top-tier malls this year has made it difficult for analysts and investors to make more accurate calls about market conditions. "The extended period of inactivity is disquieting," said real-estate research firm Green Street Advisors in a recent note. "Mall REITs should be more active in selling assets given the massive current discounts at which they trade relative to our net asset value estimates."

Australian investment manager QIC's recent acquisition of Forest City Realty Trust's stake in 10 regional malls should have created some buzz since the assets sold at a capitalization rate, a measure of yield, of 5% to 6%. But the deal had been agreed upon some time ago and likely isn't indicative of the current transaction environment, Green Street said.

For REITs, there is the added burden of making sure any sales proceeds can be deployed for other uses quickly, given their inability to hoard cash. These landlords are hesitant to sell in part because of the lack of attractive assets to buy as well as a general reluctance to do share buybacks.

"The question is, where are they going to invest the capital?" said Alexander Goldfarb, managing director at Sandler O'Neill + Partners.

One exception would be New York City's largest office landlord, SL Green Realty Corp., which has been active on the deals front this year. It sold 16 Court St. in Brooklyn in August and is currently in negotiations to sell its interest in the office building on 1515 Broadway. Alongside private investment manager RXR Realty, it also recently acquired a 49% stake in Worldwide Plaza, a Class A office building in Manhattan valued at $1.73 billion.

But SL Green also spent roughly $102 million in the third quarter to buy back shares under its share-repurchase plan, a larger-than-expected repurchase. The REIT has spent $350 million in share buybacks so far.

Pro Cyber
Cybersecurity Investors Favor Cloud Security, Artificial Intelligence 

By Jeff Stone 
1,473 words
25 October 2017
12:36
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Investors have spent years pouring venture capital into a diverse portfolio of cybersecurity startups that specialize in a range of different products.

As one massive data breach after another highlights the need for more effective security solutions, investment has flowed toward companies that promise to secure data stored in the cloud and identity management firms offering technology to verify that online accounts correspond with the correct person. Now, venture capitalists say the next generation of investment will go toward artificial intelligence providers and similar services that use automation to detect the kind of malicious activity human operators can't stop.

Cybersecurity firms have taken on funding for a generation, since web users began to understand that every internet connection comes with some sort of risk attached. Initially, companies that promised a full cybersecurity platform--such as Symantec Corp. and McAfee Inc.--dominated the market by offering a suite of services centered around their antivirus software.

As digital threats have grown increasingly complicated, though, and the cost of security protecting data has climbed, a generation of startup companies has risen to prominence. Rather than focusing on antivirus software, the most successful startups have sought to differentiate themselves by specializing in clients' specific demands such as password authentication, endpoint anti-malware products, and other areas.

Cloud security startups received the most venture capital funding between 2012 and the second quarter of 2017, according to a WSJ Pro analysis of funding data, while investors say their focus is now shifting toward artificial intelligence firms.

Twenty of the top 100 offered some form of cloud security, the most of any category, while 18 companies advertised their product as "authentication," a category that ranges from identity management to log-in technology and password security. While A.I. companies don't hold a prominent place on the list, venture capitalists say that area will be an intense focus in the future.

A more complete breakdown of the 100 most funded organizations, based on data provided by Dow Jones' VentureSource research service, is available below. Note: various companies included in the top 100 were known for multiple offerings--cloud security and authentication, for instance--and were counted accordingly, creating a data set of 107 services.

Cloud Security: 20 companies

Definition: Companies that provide secure storage in the cloud.

Authentication: 18 companies

Definition: Identity management firms that promise to ensure that users are who they say.

Endpoint: 15 companies

Definition: Any service that promises to stop malicious software before it can infect clients' computers or corporate networks.

Network Security: 15 companies

Definition: Firms that specialize in firewalls, domain name servers and other infrastructure protection techniques.

Security Services: 12 companies

Definition: Any company that offers specialized services, such as changing clients' source code to stay ahead of attackers or deception techniques meant to uncover hackers.

Financial Technology: 8 companies

Definition: Enterprises that aim to eliminate digital fraud, so-called bot scamming, and the like.

Mobile Security: 6 companies

Definition: Companies that promise to protect smartphones and other portable connected devices.

Risk Assessment: 6 companies

Definition: Firms that offer independent security ratings and/or penetration testing, which occurs when third parties test an organization's vulnerabilities before hackers can.

Email Security: 4 companies

Definition: A category primarily populated by anti-phishing and email management firms.

Artificial Intelligence: 3 companies

Definition: Companies that rely on automation, algorithms and so-called machine learning to detect anomalies, analyze malicious code and stop malware before infections occur.

Up into the Cloud

Cloud security providers have taken on so much venture funding in part because of their willingness to store and secure clients' data at a fraction of the price it would cost a company to assume those responsibilities on its own, investors said.

"The move to cloud architecture is driving major spending in security," said Asheem Chandna, a partner at the venture firm Greylock Partners, estimating that between $50 billion and $70 billion is currently being spent on cloud storage, resulting in more spending on securing that data. "Over the next seven years I think there will be $500 billion on the cloud. That's a tremendous transfer in spending in the next few years."

Providers like Zscaler Inc. and Netskope Inc. have risen to prominence as leaders in a crowded field of companies that aim to provide secure data storage.

The expected release of billions of internet-connected devices such as home appliances and innocuous office devices could be another boon to cloud security providers, as small startups seek a partner to store their data safely.

The Future of Security

The funding gold rush has led to an influx of security companies that often offer services with little to no difference from the competition. It's a situation that investors say they aim to resolve not by examining the A.I. and machine learning algorithms, but by assessing how that advanced technology is combined with other services and presented to the customer. For an A.I. product to be valuable in the eyes of an investor, it will need to be easy to use.

"You can't be a modern-day security company if you're not using A.I. and machine learning to solve problems," said Ping Li, a partner at the venture capital firm Accel Partners. "We look for how companies actually build an application around that."

Simply put, A.I. firms aim to build technology that automatically detects anomalies in large sets of data. Potential red flags might include a disgruntled employee trying to steal corporate intellectual property, nation state hackers seeking to access a company's health records, or a false positive which wrongly indicates something is wrong. Currently, sifting through millions of data points and false alarms presents a major problem for non-security companies unable to fill an "https://www.csoonline.com/article/3200024/security/cybersecurity-labor-crunch-to-hit-35-million-unfilled-jobs-by-2021.html" expected to remain open through 2021.

Cylance Inc. and Darktrace Ltd., which specialize in A.I. have earned reported valuations of over $1 billion and $825 million, respectively, though investors estimated that dozens of smaller A.I. firms have taken on private funding in recent months not covered by VentureSource data.

"There's as much marketing as anything else going into these but we're seeing that levels of automation are really essential to an effective cybersecurity posture," said Robert Ackerman, founder and managing director of the cybersecurity venture firm Allegis Capital, citing the 2014 breach at Target Corp. in which a true anomaly was buried by false positives.

"When Target identified that something was wrong, they were overwhelmed with information. When something like that goes wrong, the malware is the smallest needle in the biggest haystack of data imaginable. The underlying thing we need is for automation to identify those problems."

Market Contraction

Investors predicted a kind of survival-of-the-fittest scenario in which leaders emerge from crowded cybersecurity fields. Over the next 18 to 24 months, experts said, public companies will merge with other public companies while also acquiring smaller firms. Venky Ganesan, managing director at Menlo Ventures, predicted a rash of bankruptcies among the "many firms that are actually just ideas that are masquerading as companies."

The consolidation process has already begun. In August, the risk management firm Redowl Analytics was acquired by the network security company Forcepoint, which itself is owned by the defense contractor Raytheon Co. Before that, the software giant CA Technologies acquired the application security firm Veracode, and a range of similar transactions have occurred in recent months.

Those kinds of deals, which are expected to continue, help clarify for investors a company's true value. When CA Technologies acquired Veracode for $614 million, for instance, that figure offered insight into the potential value of other app security companies.

"Cyber has been such a rich area of investment because the smaller companies have the ability to disrupt the larger players, and customers are willing to bet on the smaller companies because the nature of the threat is so acute," said Ted Schlein, general partner at venture-capital firm Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, a major technology investor.

One reason smaller security startups are so well funded is because customers are willing to trust new firms that demonstrate they have new capabilities.

The risk of experiencing a breach is just too much, Mr. Schlein said.

"The world of cybersecurity is hardcore," he said. "Chief executives lose their job, companies can lose billions in market cap, and it's such a topical issue right now that it's all thrust into the media very quickly."

(Jeff Stone writes exclusively for WSJ Pro Cybersecurity. He previously covered privacy, international hacking groups, bug bounties, and a range of related topics at media outlets including the Christian Science Monitor and the International Business Times. Write to Jeff at jeff.stone@wsj.com)

VC firm targets US cannabis market

AK THOMSON 
666 words
24 October 2017
Financial Times
FTFT

14

Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Financial services. Pot of gold Casa Verde Capital focuses exclusively on ancillary side of a fast-growing industry

When Karan Wadhera abandoned a successful career as an investment banker in Asia to plunge into North America's cannabis industry, former colleagues could have been forgiven for thinking he was smoking it himself.

But for Mr Wadhera, Indian by birth but who has a US education and entertainment-industry connections in Los Angeles, the move was an obvious one. "By 2013, it was clear that there was a business opportunity," he says. "It was a great moment to get proactive."

Today, Mr Wadhera is managing partner of Casa Verde Capital, a venture capital firm launched in 2015 that invests in technology companies serving the cannabis industry.

With an initial fund of $25m, Casa Verde, which counts the rapper Snoop Dogg among its four founding partners, is small compared with the likes of Privateer Holdings or Tuatara Capital, two of the US's biggest cannabis-focused investment companies.

But it has set itself apart by investing exclusively in the ancillary side of the market. With most of its investments made this year, Casa Verde has typically sunk $500,000 initially   often increasing it later   into each of seven start-ups in return for an equity stake ranging from 5 per cent to 10 per cent.

It has invested in Green Bits, which makes payments terminals that can handle the exacting compliance requirements for cannabis sales in retail dispensaries. Mr Wadhera says that their machines, in roughly 800 locations so far, are on course to process $1.7bn in payments this year alone.

It has also invested in LeafLink, a business-to-business platform that provides a market for dispensary owners to buy inventory from cannabis brands. In April 2016, when it started, the platform processed $12,000 in transactions; last month, it processed almost $14m.

Casa Verde plans to raise "a much larger fund" over the next year or two, Mr Wadhera says. The portfolio-building comes as US states are fast adopting more flexible laws on cannabis. At least 29 states have now legalised marijuana in some form. In January, California is set to legalise recreational marijuana, creating what is expected to be the world's single-largest legal cannabis market.

Roy Bingham, founder of the cannabis data company BDS Analytics, estimates that the legal North American market last year was worth $6.7bn in sales   a figure he expects to grow to $22.6bn by 2021. He also says that the market is evolving beyond recognition.

"A lot of people still tend to think about bags of weed," he says. "But half of the legal market is now branded consumer products sold in packaging similar to what you might buy at Walgreens."

Despite the market's rapid growth, Casa Verde's strategy of targeting ancillary companies speaks to two related structural issues that the industry faces.

The first is that cannabis remains illegal under federal law   a fact that poses potentially onerous risk even as the federal government has generally allowed businesses to operate as long as they comply with local laws.

"None of our investments touch the plant," explains Mr Wadhera. "There was some level of risk mitigation in that decision so we would probably not be under the same level of scrutiny [as hands-on cannabis companies]."

More important is the potential that ancillary businesses have for scaling up given that they are not bound by locally issued operating licences and regulations, which place strict limits on how and where producers and retailers can operate.

"We only wanted to be in businesses that could touch the entire industry," says Mr Wadhera. "If you have a friend with a licence for Santa Monica, you are very limited. You just can't scale in the way you might want to."

Peirce, Jackson Edge Closer to SEC Posts

The Securities and Exchange Commission is still operating with only three of its five top posts filled, but perhaps not for much longer.

On Tuesday, the Senate Banking Committee is holding a 10 a.m. hearing on the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/white-house-to-nominate-hester-peirce-as-republican-sec-commissioner-1500417225" and Democrat Robert Jackson to join the commission an important step in the nominees' path to confirmation. Once approved by the panel, their nominations move to the full Senate.

SEC nominees are traditionally presented to Congress as a Republican-Democratic pair to preserve a partisan balance on the commission. Ms. Peirce has been tapped for a GOP slot on the commission before, during the Obama administration, but her nomination was one of many that stalled as Congress focused on other priorities. She is a researcher at the right-leaning Mercatus Center at George Mason University , and "https://www.wsj.com/articles/sec-nominee-hester-peirce-is-outspoken-critic-of-post-crisis-regulation-1457978884" such as the creation of the Financial Stability Oversight Council .

The Democratic nominee this time around, Robert Jackson, is "http://www.law.nyu.edu/news/rob-jackson-joining-nyu-law-faculty-corporations-securities-sec" who has written on securities topics such as executive compensation and corporate governance. He was among a group of academics that "https://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-eyes-columbia-professor-for-sec-seat-1501271290" to require public companies to disclose their political-spending activities.

If confirmed, they will join Chairman Jay Clayton, an independent; Michael Piwowar, a Republican; and Kara Stein , a Democrat. A commission with all five seats filled should make it easier for Mr. Clayton to carry out his agenda of reducing the regulatory burden for public companies.

WSJ Pro: Number of Eurozone Banks Continues to Fall

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/number-of-eurozone-banks-continues-to-fall-1508748857" continued to decline last year, while lenders' capital buffers improved, data from the European Central Bank showed Monday.

On an unconsolidated basis, the number of credit institutions at the end of last year was 5,073 compared with 5,474 in the previous year. On a consolidated basis, meaning that banks belonging to the same group count as one, there were 2,290 credit institutions at the end of last year versus 2,379 a year earlier and 2,904 in 2008. Bank executives have said that increased digitization leads to banks laying off workers. The ECB data showed that in 2016 alone nearly 7,000 bank branches were closed.

The ECB data also show, however, that the remaining banks are becoming healthier. Banks' median phased-in common equity Tier 1 capital ratio, a measure of bank health, stood at 15.4% last year, up from 14.4% one year earlier.

What to Watch: Peirce, Jackson Edge Closer to SEC Posts

Trump Administration Increases Heat on CFPB's Arbitration Rule

Peirce, Jackson Edge Closer to SEC Posts

The Securities and Exchange Commission is still operating with only three of its five top posts filled, but perhaps not for much longer.

On Tuesday, the Senate Banking Committee is holding a 10 a.m. hearing on the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/white-house-to-nominate-hester-peirce-as-republican-sec-commissioner-1500417225" and Democrat Robert Jackson to join the commission an important step in the nominees' path to confirmation.
